The viral infection remains latent, and disease may recur years later as shingles.\n\nThe TCI Ministry of Health strongly advises persons affected with chickenpox to remain at home during their sick leave period to prevent further spread of this illness within the community and schools.\n\n[Byline: Robert Herriman]\n\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Varicella-zoster virus, a member of the herpesvirus family is the causative agent for chickenpox. Humans are the only reservoir of the virus, and disease occurs only in humans. After primary infection as varicella (chickenpox), the virus remains dormant in the sensory-nerve ganglia and can reactivate at a later time, causing herpes zoster (shingles).\n\nVaricella occurs worldwide. In temperate climates, varicella tends to be a childhood disease, with peak incidence among preschool and school-aged children during late winter and early spring. In these countries, less than 5 percent of adults are susceptible to varicella. In tropical climates, the highest incidence was described in the driest, coolest months; overall, infection tends to be acquired later in childhood, resulting in higher susceptibility among adults than in temperate climates, especially in less densely populated areas.\n\nAll people, including those traveling or living abroad, should be assessed for varicella immunity, and those who do not have evidence of immunity or contraindications to vaccination should receive age-appropriate vaccination. Vaccination against varicella is not a requirement for entry into any country (including the United States), but people who do not have evidence of immunity should be considered at risk for varicella during international travel.\n\nVaricella vaccine contains live, attenuated varicella-zoster virus. Single-antigen varicella vaccine is licensed for people aged 12 months and older, and the combination measles-mumps-rubella-varicella (MMRV) vaccine is licensed only for children 1-12 years. CDC recommends varicella vaccination for all people aged 12 months and older without evidence of immunity to varicella who do not have contraindications to the vaccine: 1 dose for children aged 1-4 years and 2 doses for people aged 4 years and older. The minimum interval between doses is 3 months for children aged less than 13 years and 4 weeks for people aged 13 years and older. Contraindications for vaccination include allergy to vaccine components, immune-compromising conditions or treatments, and pregnancy. When evidence of immunity is uncertain, a possible history of varicella is not a contraindication to varicella vaccination. Vaccine effectiveness is approximately 80 percent after 1 dose and 95 percent after 2 doses.\n\n(Excerpted and edited from http://wwwnc.cdc.gov/travel/yellowbook/2016/infectious-diseases-related-to-travel/varicella-chickenpox)\n\nMaps of the Turks and Caicos Islands may be accessed at http://www.worldatlas.com/webimage/countrys/namerica/caribb/tc.htm and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/48358. - Replies received were also forwarded\nthis morning directly to the inquirer. Sincere thanks to all the\nsubscribers who replied so promptly and with a wealth of information. - Mod.]\n\n[1]\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 1996 12:57:17 GMT-10\nFrom: Richard Doherty <Richard.Doherty@med.monash.edu.au>\nConventional wisdom is that infants born between five days before and\ntwo days after a mother comes out in a varicella rash are at risk of\nsevere varicella, because they have been exposed in-utero without\nhaving remained there long enough to benefit from the emerging\nmaternal immune response. Whether maternal immunoglobulin has anything\nto do with it is not clear to me given the short time frame and\nthe fact that IgM traditionally does not cross the placenta.\nI would interpret the appearance of the baby's rash at that point as\nbeing indicative of transplacental spread of VZV [varicella-zoster\nvirus] during the initial low level viraemic phase which occurs\nat about three days after initial infection (it is this low level wave\nthat you are trying to block with the neutralising antibody in\nV-ZIG [varicella-zoster immune globulin] when you give it to exposed at risk\nindividuals). Spread at this stage would seem to be a little unusual,\nbut by no means inconceivable. If this is so, then the baby may\nwell have been exposed to VZV for long enough to benefit from whatever\nspecific or non specific responses might have been transferred\ntrans-placentally, but perhaps more importantly, might have also been\nable to mount a response itself.\nThese postulates notwithstanding, I would have a very low threshold\nfor starting this infant on intravenous acyclovir, and would have expect\nV-ZIG to have little if any effect on the clinical course of the\nillness.\nTwo references from a quick search are appended, but the chapter on\nVZV infection in Remington and Klein, \"Infectious Diseases of the\nFetus and Newborn Infant\" is likely to give you all the info you need\nand all the key references.\n89343356\nOutcome in newborn babies given anti-varicella-zoster immunoglobulin after\nperinatal maternal infection with varicella-zoster virus.\nMiller E; Cradock-Watson JE; Ridehalgh MK\nLancet (ENGLAND) Aug 12 1989 2 (8659) p371-3 ISSN: 0140-6736\n281 newborn babies whose mothers had chickenpox and 25 whose mothers had\nherpes zoster during the perinatal period were investigated. IgG antibody was\npresent at birth in all babies born more than 7 days after the onset of\nmaternal chickenpox. When the mother's rash appeared 7-3 days before delivery\nprogressively fewer babies were born with antibody, and no infant born less\nthan 3 days after the onset had antibody at birth. Infants were given 100 or\n250 mg of anti-varicella-zoster immunoglobulin (VZIG) shortly after birth or\nthe onset of post-natal maternal chickenpox. 1 infant died, without neonatal\nvaricella. Of the 280 surviving infants 169 (60%) were infected--134 (48%)\nwith chickenpox and 35 (13%) without clinical features. The clinical attack\nrate was highest (60%) in infants whose mothers had chickenpox between 7 days\nbefore and 7 days after delivery. Chickenpox was severe in 19 infants, 16 of\nwhom were exposed to maternal chickenpox between 4 days before and 2 days after\ndelivery. There was no evidence that a severe outcome was associated with\ntransplacentally acquired infection. Perinatal maternal herpes zoster did not\ncause neonatal infection. VZIG should be given to infants at risk, including\nthose whose mothers have chickenpox during the last 7 days of pregnancy.\n\n89132297\nVaricella-zoster virus infections in pregnancy [see comments]\nFox GN; Strangarity JW\nAm Fam Physician (UNITED STATES) Feb 1989 39 (2) p89-98 ISSN: 0002-838X\nNote: Comment in: Am Fam Physician 1989 Aug;40(2):55\nVaricella-zoster virus can cause a distinct congenital syndrome, a\npotentially fatal neonatal infection and life-threatening maternal illness.\nPhysicians can reduce morbidity from these conditions by advising nonimmune\npregnant women to avoid exposure to chickenpox and herpes zoster and, when\nindicated, by promptly administering varicella-zoster immune globulin. When\nprevention fails, acyclovir may be effective therapy. (24 References)\nBest wishes\n--\nRichard Doherty, Professor of Paediatrics\nDepartment of Paediatrics, Faculty of Medicine, Monash University\nMonash Medical Centre, Clayton, VIC 3168.\nPhone Int'l +61 3 9550 4490 Fax Int'l +61 3 9550 4124\ne-mail: Richard.Doherty@med.monash.edu.au\n...........................................\n[2]\nFrom: \"Dykewicz, Clare\" <cad3@NIP1.EM.CDC.GOV>\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 96 10:59:00 EST\nNeonatal varicella may be biphasic. Infants may have a few lesions, show\nimprovement for several days, then develop a generalized rash. Some\nnewborns may develop prolonged varicella with new lesions occurring into the\nsecond week of life. Possible complications of neonatal varicella include\nbacterial superinfection, and pneumonia. About 30% of newborns born to\nmothers who develop varicella 5 days before, or 2 days after delivery will\ndie of neonatal varicella. Death typically occurs from pneumonia or\ndisseminated visceral involvement. VZIG [varicella-zoster immune\nglobulin] administration can decrease morbidity and mortality.\nThe continued temperature instability of this child is concerning,\nand may signify ongoing viral or bacterial infection.\nClose observation is warranted. Depending on the clinical\ncourse, intravenous acyclovir may be indicated.\n--\nClare Dykewicz\nChief, Mumps, Rubella, Varicella Activity\nNational Immunization Program, CDC\ne-mail: cad3@NIP1.EM.CDC.GOV\n.............................................................................\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Sincere thanks to all the\nsubscribers who replied so promptly and with a wealth of information. - Mod.]\n\n[1]\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 1996 12:57:17 GMT-10\nFrom: Richard Doherty <Richard.Doherty@med.monash.edu.au>\nConventional wisdom is that infants born between five days before and\ntwo days after a mother comes out in a varicella rash are at risk of\nsevere varicella, because they have been exposed in-utero without\nhaving remained there long enough to benefit from the emerging\nmaternal immune response. Whether maternal immunoglobulin has anything\nto do with it is not clear to me given the short time frame and\nthe fact that IgM traditionally does not cross the placenta.\nI would interpret the appearance of the baby's rash at that point as\nbeing indicative of transplacental spread of VZV [varicella-zoster\nvirus] during the initial low level viraemic phase which occurs\nat about three days after initial infection (it is this low level wave\nthat you are trying to block with the neutralising antibody in\nV-ZIG [varicella-zoster immune globulin] when you give it to exposed at risk\nindividuals). Spread at this stage would seem to be a little unusual,\nbut by no means inconceivable. If this is so, then the baby may\nwell have been exposed to VZV for long enough to benefit from whatever\nspecific or non specific responses might have been transferred\ntrans-placentally, but perhaps more importantly, might have also been\nable to mount a response itself.\nThese postulates notwithstanding, I would have a very low threshold\nfor starting this infant on intravenous acyclovir, and would have expect\nV-ZIG to have little if any effect on the clinical course of the\nillness.\nTwo references from a quick search are appended, but the chapter on\nVZV infection in Remington and Klein, \"Infectious Diseases of the\nFetus and Newborn Infant\" is likely to give you all the info you need\nand all the key references.\n89343356\nOutcome in newborn babies given anti-varicella-zoster immunoglobulin after\nperinatal maternal infection with varicella-zoster virus.\nMiller E; Cradock-Watson JE; Ridehalgh MK\nLancet (ENGLAND) Aug 12 1989 2 (8659) p371-3 ISSN: 0140-6736\n281 newborn babies whose mothers had chickenpox and 25 whose mothers had\nherpes zoster during the perinatal period were investigated. IgG antibody was\npresent at birth in all babies born more than 7 days after the onset of\nmaternal chickenpox. When the mother's rash appeared 7-3 days before delivery\nprogressively fewer babies were born with antibody, and no infant born less\nthan 3 days after the onset had antibody at birth. Infants were given 100 or\n250 mg of anti-varicella-zoster immunoglobulin (VZIG) shortly after birth or\nthe onset of post-natal maternal chickenpox. 1 infant died, without neonatal\nvaricella. Of the 280 surviving infants 169 (60%) were infected--134 (48%)\nwith chickenpox and 35 (13%) without clinical features. The clinical attack\nrate was highest (60%) in infants whose mothers had chickenpox between 7 days\nbefore and 7 days after delivery. Chickenpox was severe in 19 infants, 16 of\nwhom were exposed to maternal chickenpox between 4 days before and 2 days after\ndelivery. There was no evidence that a severe outcome was associated with\ntransplacentally acquired infection. Perinatal maternal herpes zoster did not\ncause neonatal infection. VZIG should be given to infants at risk, including\nthose whose mothers have chickenpox during the last 7 days of pregnancy.\n\n89132297\nVaricella-zoster virus infections in pregnancy [see comments]\nFox GN; Strangarity JW\nAm Fam Physician (UNITED STATES) Feb 1989 39 (2) p89-98 ISSN: 0002-838X\nNote: Comment in: Am Fam Physician 1989 Aug;40(2):55\nVaricella-zoster virus can cause a distinct congenital syndrome, a\npotentially fatal neonatal infection and life-threatening maternal illness.\nPhysicians can reduce morbidity from these conditions by advising nonimmune\npregnant women to avoid exposure to chickenpox and herpes zoster and, when\nindicated, by promptly administering varicella-zoster immune globulin. When\nprevention fails, acyclovir may be effective therapy. (24 References)\nBest wishes\n--\nRichard Doherty, Professor of Paediatrics\nDepartment of Paediatrics, Faculty of Medicine, Monash University\nMonash Medical Centre, Clayton, VIC 3168.\nPhone Int'l +61 3 9550 4490 Fax Int'l +61 3 9550 4124\ne-mail: Richard.Doherty@med.monash.edu.au\n...........................................\n[2]\nFrom: \"Dykewicz, Clare\" <cad3@NIP1.EM.CDC.GOV>\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 96 10:59:00 EST\nNeonatal varicella may be biphasic. Infants may have a few lesions, show\nimprovement for several days, then develop a generalized rash. Some\nnewborns may develop prolonged varicella with new lesions occurring into the\nsecond week of life. Possible complications of neonatal varicella include\nbacterial superinfection, and pneumonia. About 30% of newborns born to\nmothers who develop varicella 5 days before, or 2 days after delivery will\ndie of neonatal varicella. Death typically occurs from pneumonia or\ndisseminated visceral involvement. VZIG [varicella-zoster immune\nglobulin] administration can decrease morbidity and mortality.\nThe continued temperature instability of this child is concerning,\nand may signify ongoing viral or bacterial infection.\nClose observation is warranted. Depending on the clinical\ncourse, intravenous acyclovir may be indicated.\n--\nClare Dykewicz\nChief, Mumps, Rubella, Varicella Activity\nNational Immunization Program, CDC\ne-mail: cad3@NIP1.EM.CDC.GOV\n.............................................................................\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. (2)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2187698", "date": "1996-01-10 23:50:00", "main_text": "From: Debbi Moore <LODMOORE@ihc.com>\nDate: Tue, 09 Jan 1996 16:33:24 -0700\nVARICELLA, NEONATAL ?: CASE REPORT & REQUEST FOR INFO\n=====================================================\nWe have had a women deliver here at our hospital\nwith active chickenpox. The patient noted skin\neruptions beginning 3 to 3 1/2 days prior to\ndelivery.\nPresently, at 8 to 10 hours of age, increasing skin\neruptions have been noted on the infant, with a\ntypical maculopapular appearance. Baby is eating\nwell, but has had some temperature instability.\nCBC, blood glucose, platelets are all WNL, blood\nculture is pending.\nThe sources I have checked are vague\nconcerning placental transmission of varicella and\non varicella developing in an infant this soon after\ndelivery.\nAny comments would be helpful.\n..................\nDate: Wed, 10 Jan 1996 09:18:15 -0700\nUpdate on our case:\nThe infant received VZIG at approx. 12 hours after\nbirth. Mom has been on Zovirax since appearance\nof her skin eruptions.\nThis morning the rash on the infant has resolved.\nInfant still has an unstable temp., otherwise doing\nwell.\n--\nDebbi Moore\nInfection Control\ne-mail: LODMOORE@IHC.COM\n..........................................................................\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The patient noted skin\neruptions beginning 3 to 3 1/2 days prior to\ndelivery.\nPresently, at 8 to 10 hours of age, increasing skin\neruptions have been noted on the infant, with a\ntypical maculopapular appearance. Baby is eating\nwell, but has had some temperature instability.\nCBC, blood glucose, platelets are all WNL, blood\nculture is pending.\nThe sources I have checked are vague\nconcerning placental transmission of varicella and\non varicella developing in an infant this soon after\ndelivery.\nAny comments would be helpful.\n..................\nDate: Wed, 10 Jan 1996 09:18:15 -0700\nUpdate on our case:\nThe infant received VZIG at approx. 12 hours after\nbirth. Mom has been on Zovirax since appearance\nof her skin eruptions.\nThis morning the rash on the infant has resolved.\nInfant still has an unstable temp., otherwise doing\nwell.\n--\nDebbi Moore\nInfection Control\ne-mail: LODMOORE@IHC.COM\n..........................................................................\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed.
